- Recognize *mitigation patterns*: serialization filters in Java, or migration to safer serializers in .NET. ţłÇciteţłéturn28search1ţłéturn28search29ţłéturn28search12ţłü

**Examples.**

```java
// Java (unsafe)
ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(socket.getInputStream());
Object obj = ois.readObject();
```

```csharp
// C# (unsafe)
var bf = new BinaryFormatter();
var obj = bf.Deserialize(networkStream);
```

```python
# Python (unsafe)
obj = pickle.loads(request.data)
```

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Avoid unsafe deserialization for untrusted data. Where legacy constraints exist: apply **serialization filters** (Java), migrate away from `BinaryFormatter` (.NET), and use data-only formats with strict schemas. ţłÇciteţłéturn28search1ţłéturn28search9ţłéturn28search0ţłéturn22search1ţłü

### Untrusted input reaches query construction and execution

**Definition.** Untrusted input is incorporated into a database or directory query (SQL/NoSQL/LDAP/etc.) in a way that changes query structure, enabling injection. ţłÇciteţłéturn22search2ţłéturn8search36ţłü

**Rationale.** Injection often provides direct read/write access to sensitive data and can become RCE depending on DB extensions or chained weaknesses. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search36ţłéturn22search2ţłéturn5search7ţłü

**Evidence of validation.** SQL injection is CWE-89 and is consistently emphasized in OWASP guidance and top weakness lists. ţłÇciteţłéturn22search2ţłéturn8search36ţłéturn5search7ţłü SATE VI contains empirical discussion that tool precision/recall rankings vary by weakness types and that warning overlap/combination is a useful dimension, aligning with how injection rules are typically implemented across tools. ţłÇciteţłéturn17view1ţłéturn18view0ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Very broad; every language has DB client libraries, and the principal risk factor is **string building of query syntax** rather than parameterization. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search36ţłéturn22search2ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives rise when analyzers canÔÇÖt model sanitizers/ORM escaping, or when query strings are partially constant and partially dynamic. False negatives are common where queries are constructed indirectly (query builders, templated SQL, string fragments across functions) beyond the analyzerÔÇÖs interprocedural limits. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view1ţłéturn20view0ţłéturn13academia29ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Flag string concatenation/formatting feeding `execute`, `query`, `Statement.execute*`, etc. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search36ţłéturn22search2ţłü  
- Treat ÔÇťparameterized/prepared statement APIsÔÇŁ as preferred sinks; downgrade severity when all user-controlled values are bound parameters. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search36ţłü  
- Taint mode: sources Ôćĺ string ops Ôćĺ query execution sink; allow known safe query builder APIs as sanitizers only if they enforce parameterization. ţłÇciteţłéturn7search14ţłéturn8search36ţłü

**Examples.**

```java
// Java (unsafe)
String q = "SELECT * FROM users WHERE name = '" + name + "'";
stmt.executeQuery(q);
```

```csharp
// C# (unsafe)
var q = "SELECT * FROM users WHERE name = '" + name + "'";
new SqlCommand(q, conn).ExecuteReader();
```

```python
# Python (safer)
cur.execute("SELECT * FROM users WHERE name = ?", (name,))
```

OWASP recommends parameterized queries / prepared statements as a primary preventive measure. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search36ţłü

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Use parameterized queries/ORM bindings; reserve dynamic SQL for controlled, allowlisted fragments (e.g., sort columns) and validate with allowlists. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search36ţłéturn8search4ţłü

### Untrusted input reaches filesystem paths and restricted-directory operations

**Definition.** External input is used to construct a pathname intended to remain under a restricted directory, but special elements allow resolution outside that directory (path traversal). ţłÇciteţłéturn25search0ţłéturn8search0ţłü

**Rationale.** Path traversal can expose configuration secrets, source code, credentials, or enable file overwrite in upload/extraction flows (ÔÇťzip slipÔÇŁ class). ţłÇciteţłéturn8search0ţłéturn25search0ţłéturn5search7ţłü

**Evidence of validation.** CWE-22 defines the class; OWASP provides attack descriptions and mitigations; CWE-23 mitigation guidance includes canonicalization examples across multiple languages, reinforcing cross-language generalizability. ţłÇciteţłéturn25search0ţłéturn8search0ţłéturn8search20ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Broad. Canonicalization and base-directory enforcement exist in all target languages (though APIs differ). ţłÇciteţłéturn8search20ţłéturn25search0ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives occur when tools cannot prove base-directory enforcement (e.g., canonicalization + prefix check) or when path inputs are internal but modeled as external. False negatives occur when path construction spans multiple functions/files or when framework-specific ÔÇťstatic file servingÔÇŁ paths arenÔÇÖt modeled as sinks. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn20view0ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Sink set: file read/write/open, archive extraction targets, static file serving root joins. ţłÇciteţłéturn25search0ţłéturn8search0ţłü  
- Pattern: `join(base, userPart)` without canonicalization+enforcement. CWE-23 explicitly recommends canonicalization functions (e.g., Java `getCanonicalPath`, .NET `GetFullPath`). ţłÇciteţłéturn8search20ţłéturn25search0ţłü  
- Positive pattern to recognize: canonicalize Ôćĺ verify canonical path starts with canonical base directory Ôćĺ operate. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search20ţłéturn8search4ţłü  

**Examples.**

```python
# Python (unsafe)
path = os.path.join(BASE_DIR, user_filename)
with open(path, "rb") as f:
    return f.read()
```

```java
// Java (safer pattern)
Path base = Paths.get(BASE_DIR).toRealPath();
Path p = base.resolve(userFilename).normalize();
if (!p.startsWith(base)) throw new SecurityException();
byte[] bytes = Files.readAllBytes(p);
```

(CWE guidance explicitly points to canonicalization approaches across languages.) ţłÇciteţłéturn8search20ţłéturn25search0ţłü

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Prefer indirect references (IDs) instead of raw filenames; otherwise canonicalize and enforce base directory, and avoid using user-provided paths for security decisions without allowlists. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search0ţłéturn8search4ţłéturn8search20ţłü

### Untrusted input reaches outbound URL fetching

**Definition.** Server fetches a resource from a URL supplied or influenced by an attacker, enabling server-side request forgery (SSRF) to internal networks/metadata services. ţłÇciteţłéturn22search3ţłéturn8search1ţłéturn8search5ţłü

**Rationale.** SSRF exploits the serverÔÇÖs network position and credentials to access otherwise unreachable resources (internal services, cloud metadata endpoints), bypassing perimeter controls. ţłÇciteţłéturn22search3ţłéturn8search5ţłü

**Evidence of validation.** CWE-918 defines SSRF, and OWASP provides dedicated prevention guidance emphasizing allowlists and URL enforcement, including defenses against redirect and DNS rebinding considerations. ţłÇciteţłéturn22search3ţłéturn8search1ţłéturn8search5ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Broad across web-capable stacks; all target languages have HTTP clients and URL parsers, and the core indicator is identical: **user-controlled URL Ôćĺ fetch**. ţłÇciteţłéturn22search3ţłéturn8search1ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives occur if tools lack context about ÔÇťinternal-only allowlistsÔÇŁ or if a URL is validated elsewhere out-of-model. False negatives are common where URLs are transformed (redirectors, shorteners) or where SSRF is enabled through secondary channels (headers, open redirects). ţłÇciteţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn8search1ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Sources: request parameters that look like URLs, hostnames, callbacks. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search1ţłéturn8search4ţłü  
- Sinks: outbound HTTP fetch APIs.  
- Required guards (static recognizers): allowlist enforcement on scheme/host/port; disable redirects; resolve DNS and verify IP ranges where feasible. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search5ţłéturn8search1ţłü  

**Examples.**

```javascript
// Node.js (unsafe)
const r = await fetch(req.query.url);
```

```python
# Python (safer-ish skeleton)
u = urllib.parse.urlparse(url)
if u.scheme not in {"https"}: raise ValueError()
if u.hostname not in ALLOWED_HOSTS: raise ValueError()
resp = requests.get(url, allow_redirects=False, timeout=3)
```

OWASP explicitly recommends positive allowlists for schema/port/destination and warns about redirects and DNS rebinding/TOCTOU considerations. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search5ţłéturn8search1ţłü

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Enforce allowlists, block redirects, and separate network zones (egress controls) where possible; treat any ÔÇťfetch arbitrary URLÔÇŁ feature as high risk unless strongly justified. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search1ţłéturn8search5ţłü

### Hardcoded credentials, secrets, and embedded key material

**Definition.** Software contains hardcoded credentials (passwords, API keys, cryptographic keys/tokens) used for inbound/outbound authentication or encryption. ţłÇciteţłéturn25search2ţłéturn8search6ţłéturn8search2ţłü

**Rationale.** Hardcoded secrets are extractable from source, artifacts, or client bundles, and are hard to rotate quickly and safely after exposure. ţłÇciteţłéturn25search2ţłéturn8search2ţłéturn8search6ţłü

**Evidence of validation.** CWE-798 defines this as ÔÇťUse of Hard-coded CredentialsÔÇŁ and it appears in top weakness aggregations; OWASP provides community guidance and dedicated secrets management best practices focusing on centralized storage, auditing, and rotation. ţłÇciteţłéturn25search2ţłéturn5search7ţłéturn8search2ţłéturn8search6ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Very broad; applies uniformly across all languages because it is fundamentally about **literal secrets in code/config**. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search6ţłéturn8search2ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives can occur for test credentials, placeholders, or non-secret constants that look like tokens. False negatives occur when secrets are encoded, split across strings, generated during build, or stored in adjacent config files not scanned. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn8search2ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Pattern checks: variable names (`password`, `apiKey`, `secret`) assigned string literals; high-entropy literal detectors; known key formats. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search2ţłéturn25search2ţłü  
- Cross-file include: treat `.env`, YAML, JSON, and IaC as first-class scan targets; unify results via SARIF where possible. ţłÇciteţłéturn7search0ţłéturn8search2ţłü  

**Examples.**

```python
# Python (unsafe)
API_KEY = "sk_live_ABC123..."
```

```javascript
// JavaScript (unsafe)
const password = "P@ssw0rd!";
```

```python
# Python (preferred)
API_KEY = os.environ["API_KEY"]
```

OWASP emphasizes centralized secrets storage, provisioning, auditing, and rotation as core practices. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search2ţłéturn8search38ţłü

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Adopt managed secrets storage; enforce rotation and minimize secret reuse; treat any committed secret as a security incident requiring rotation and scope review. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search2ţłéturn8search38ţłü

### Weak crypto primitives, insecure randomness, and deprecated algorithms

**Definition.** Use of broken or risky cryptographic algorithms/protocols, insecure random number generation for security-sensitive values, or configurations that violate modern cryptographic guidance. ţłÇciteţłéturn23search2ţłéturn26search0ţłéturn26search2ţłéturn15view3ţłü

**Rationale.** Weak crypto is a ÔÇťsilent failureÔÇŁ: software often appears correct while confidentiality/integrity guarantees are degraded or void. ţłÇciteţłéturn23search2ţłéturn15view3ţłéturn8search3ţłü

**Evidence of validation.**  
- CWE-327 characterizes ÔÇťbroken or risky cryptographic algorithmÔÇŁ usage. ţłÇciteţłéturn23search2ţłü  
- ţłÇentityţłé["organization","NIST","us standards institute"]ţłü provides transition guidance for cryptographic algorithms and key lengths (SP 800ÔÇĹ131A Rev.2) and publishes TLS configuration guidance (SP 800ÔÇĹ52 Rev.2). ţłÇciteţłéturn15view3ţłéturn26search3ţłéturn26search13ţłü  
- MDN explicitly warns that `Math.random()` is not cryptographically secure and recommends Web Crypto APIs instead. ţłÇciteţłéturn26search0ţłéturn26search11ţłü  
- PythonÔÇÖs `secrets` module documentation positions it as suitable for security tokens. ţłÇciteţłéturn26search2ţłü  
- NIST has announced retirement guidance for SHAÔÇĹ1; the IETF has deprecated MD5/SHAÔÇĹ1 signature hashes for TLS 1.2 and DTLS 1.2 digital signatures. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search3ţłéturn8search19ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Broad; all languages have crypto libraries and PRNG APIs, and all have ÔÇťeasy but unsafeÔÇŁ primitives (nonÔÇĹCSPRNG PRNGs, deprecated hashes/ciphers). ţłÇciteţłéturn23search2ţłéturn26search0ţłéturn26search2ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives occur when risky algorithms are used for non-security purposes (e.g., MD5 for deduplication) but still violate policy. False negatives occur when algorithms are selected via indirection (config), or when wrappers obscure the primitive choice. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn15view3ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Banned algorithm identifiers: `MD5`, `SHA1`, `DES`, `RC4`, `ECB`, etc., mapped to policy baselines (NIST/IETF/organizational). ţłÇciteţłéturn8search19ţłéturn8search3ţłéturn15view3ţłü  
- Insecure RNG: language PRNG APIs (e.g., JavaScript `Math.random`) used for tokens/session IDs/password reset links. ţłÇciteţłéturn26search0ţłéturn26search2ţłü  
- Detect ÔÇťtoken generationÔÇŁ contexts: variable names or API callsites suggest auth/session/CSRF tokens. ţłÇciteţłéturn26search2ţłéturn8search38ţłü  

**Examples.**

```javascript
// JS (unsafe)
const token = Math.random().toString(36).slice(2);
```

```javascript
// JS (safer)
const bytes = new Uint8Array(32);
crypto.getRandomValues(bytes);
```

```python
# Python (safer)
token = secrets.token_urlsafe(32)
```

MDN warns against using `Math.random()` for security and points to `crypto.getRandomValues()`. ţłÇciteţłéturn26search0ţłéturn26search11ţłü Python documents `secrets` for security tokens. ţłÇciteţłéturn26search2ţłü

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Set a cryptographic policy baseline (e.g., NIST transition guidance for keys/algorithms; TLS configuration guidance) and enforce via static rules and code review. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view3ţłéturn26search3ţłéturn26search13ţłü

### TLS certificate validation disabled or forced-trust patterns

**Definition.** The product does not validate, or incorrectly validates, certificatesÔÇöoften via ÔÇťtrust-allÔÇŁ callbacks or explicitly disabling verification. ţłÇciteţłéturn23search3ţłéturn27search3ţłéturn27search13ţłü

**Rationale.** Disabling certificate validation undermines TLS authentication, enabling man-in-the-middle and endpoint impersonation. ţłÇciteţłéturn23search3ţłéturn27search3ţłéturn27search13ţłü

**Evidence of validation.**  
- CWE-295 defines improper certificate validation. ţłÇciteţłéturn23search3ţłü  
- MicrosoftÔÇÖs CA5359 rule explicitly flags the pattern where `ServerCertificateValidationCallback` always returns `true`, stating that any certificate will pass validation. ţłÇciteţłéturn27search3ţłü  
- NodeÔÇÖs TLS documentation explains `rejectUnauthorized` controls whether server certificates are verified and defaults to true, making `false` a clear ÔÇťdisable verificationÔÇŁ signal. ţłÇciteţłéturn27search13ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Broad: each ecosystem has a way to disable verification (callbacks, flags, environment variables). ţłÇciteţłéturn27search3ţłéturn27search13ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives are typically very low for explicit ÔÇťalways trustÔÇŁ patterns, because they are unambiguous. False negatives occur when trust is weakened indirectly (custom trust stores misconfigured) or when the analyzer does not inspect deep config wiring. ţłÇciteţłéturn27search3ţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn15view1ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- .NET: flag callbacks that return constant `true` or ignore `sslPolicyErrors`. ţłÇciteţłéturn27search3ţłü  
- Node: flag agents/options setting `rejectUnauthorized: false` or global `NODE_TLS_REJECT_UNAUTHORIZED=0` in production paths. ţłÇciteţłéturn27search13ţłéturn27search33ţłü  
- Python: flag `verify=False` where requests are security-sensitive (even if sometimes used for scraping). ţłÇciteţłéturn28search2ţłéturn27search16ţłü  

**Examples.**

```csharp
// C# (unsafe)
ServicePointManager.ServerCertificateValidationCallback =
  (sender, cert, chain, errors) => true;
```

```javascript
// Node.js (unsafe)
const agent = new https.Agent({ rejectUnauthorized: false });
```

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Disallow ÔÇťtrust-allÔÇŁ in production; support internal/self-signed deployments by distributing a proper CA chain and pinning/allowlisting where appropriate, rather than disabling verification. ţłÇciteţłéturn27search3ţłéturn27search13ţłéturn26search3ţłü

### Memory-unsafe primitives and unsafe blocks

**Definition.** Operations that can write out of bounds, use memory after free, or otherwise trigger memory corruption and undefined behavior. ţłÇciteţłéturn23search0ţłéturn23search1ţłéturn5search5ţłéturn28search24ţłü

**Rationale.** Memory corruption often enables control-flow hijack or privilege escalation when an attacker controls inputs; it remains a dominant class in ÔÇťmost dangerousÔÇŁ weakness lists. ţłÇciteţłéturn5search7ţłéturn23search0ţłéturn23search1ţłü

**Evidence of validation.** CWE-787 (out-of-bounds write) and CWE-416 (use-after-free) are defined in CWE and commonly appear in top weakness aggregations. ţłÇciteţłéturn23search0ţłéturn23search1ţłéturn5search7ţłü The ţłÇentityţłé["organization","Software Engineering Institute","cmu software engineering center"]ţłü CERT C coding standard provides guidance on safer string/formatting functions and broader secure C/C++ practices. ţłÇciteţłéturn28search7ţłéturn28search24ţłéturn28search35ţłü ţłÇentityţłé["organization","OpenSSF","open source security org"]ţłü additionally publishes a hardening guide for compiler/linker options in C/C++ toolchains to improve resilience. ţłÇciteţłéturn28search17ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Highest for C/C++; meaningful for Rust via `unsafe` blocks (which allow operations the compiler canÔÇÖt verify). Lower but not zero for managed languages (native interop, unsafe code, buffer APIs). ţłÇciteţłéturn23search0ţłéturn5search5ţłéturn5search1ţłéturn28search24ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives can be high when analyzers conservatively approximate pointer aliasing, lifetimes, and bounds; false negatives occur in highly complex control/data flows or when the bug is emergent (requires deep semantic reasoning). Tool evaluations and comparative studies repeatedly show that no tool finds all issues, especially those requiring deep understanding. ţłÇciteţłéturn6search3ţłéturn20view0ţłéturn15view1ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Denylist ÔÇťhistorically unsafeÔÇŁ C APIs (`gets`, `strcpy`, `sprintf`, etc.) and enforce safer counterparts with explicit bounds. ţłÇciteţłéturn28search20ţłéturn28search7ţłéturn28search11ţłü  
- Rust: treat any `unsafe` block as requiring justification and ensure pointer arithmetic or transmute-like operations are localized and reviewed. ţłÇciteţłéturn5search5ţłéturn5search1ţłü  
- Pattern: integer overflow used in allocation/length Ôćĺ buffer write; treat as compound risk where analyzers support it. ţłÇciteţłéturn25search3ţłéturn23search0ţłü  

**Examples.**

```c
// C (unsafe)
char buf[16];
strcpy(buf, user_input);
```

```rust
// Rust (review hotspot)
unsafe {
    *ptr.add(i) = value;
}
```

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Prefer memory-safe languages or safe subsets where feasible; otherwise, harden by (a) banning unsafe APIs, (b) enabling compiler hardening options, and (c) enforcing strict review for unsafe blocks/hot functions. ţłÇciteţłéturn28search7ţłéturn28search17ţłéturn5search5ţłü

### Unchecked return values, swallowed exceptions, and fail-open control flow

**Definition.** Software fails to check return values or mishandles exceptional conditions, potentially continuing in an unsafe state (ÔÇťfail openÔÇŁ), losing security invariants. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search2ţłéturn24search3ţłéturn24search15ţłü

**Rationale.** Security checks and validation are often implemented as ÔÇťmust succeedÔÇŁ operations. Ignoring failures turns them into advisory checks, enabling bypasses. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search2ţłéturn24search3ţłü

**Evidence of validation.**  
- CWEÔÇĹ252 (ÔÇťUnchecked Return ValueÔÇŁ) and CWEÔÇĹ703 (ÔÇťImproper Check or Handling of Exceptional ConditionsÔÇŁ) define these behaviors as weakness classes. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search2ţłéturn24search3ţłü  
- OWASPÔÇÖs Top 10 (2025) introduces ÔÇťMishandling of Exceptional ConditionsÔÇŁ as a category emphasizing that failures to prevent/detect/respond to unusual situations can lead to vulnerabilities. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search15ţłü  
- CERT guidance and secure C/C++ discussions emphasize detecting and handling input/output errors and avoiding related undefined behaviors. ţłÇciteţłéturn28search24ţłéturn28search7ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Very broad: all languages have error/exception channels, and all can suffer from ignored failures, empty catches, and ÔÇťcontinue anywayÔÇŁ logic. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search2ţłéturn24search3ţłéturn24search15ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives are moderate: not every ignored return is security relevant, and some errors are deliberately ignored. False negatives happen when ÔÇťerror significanceÔÇŁ is semantic (only dangerous for certain operations), which many analyzers approximate imperfectly. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn24search2ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Flag ignored return values from security-sensitive APIs (auth checks, signature verification, permission setting, file operations, network calls). CWEÔÇĹ252 explicitly frames unchecked returns as preventing detection of unexpected states. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search2ţłü  
- Flag empty catch blocks or blanket exception suppression around security-critical operations. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search3ţłéturn24search15ţłü  
- Require ÔÇťfail closedÔÇŁ semantics for authorization and verification functions (error Ôćĺ deny). ţłÇciteţłéturn24search3ţłéturn24search15ţłü  

**Examples.**

```go
// Go (unsafe): ignoring error can skip security enforcement
_ = os.Chmod(path, 0600)
```

```java
// Java (unsafe): swallow exception -> proceed anyway
try { verifySignature(data); } catch (Exception e) { }
process(data);
```

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Make security checks explicit and non-optional: propagate failures, log with context, and default to denial/abort in sensitive pathways. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search2ţłéturn24search15ţłéturn24search3ţłü

### Missing authentication and missing authorization gates

**Definition.** Critical functions are reachable without authentication (CWEÔÇĹ306) or without authorization checks (CWEÔÇĹ862), often due to missing middleware, missing policy checks, or confusion over trust boundaries. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search0ţłéturn24search1ţłéturn5search7ţłü

**Rationale.** These issues are ÔÇťdesign-to-implementation boundary failuresÔÇŁ: they may not look like low-level bugs but can enable direct access to privileged actions or data. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search0ţłéturn24search1ţłéturn18view0ţłü

**Evidence of validation.** Missing authentication/authorization classes appear in top weakness lists. ţłÇciteţłéturn5search7ţłéturn24search0ţłéturn24search1ţłü OWASP emphasizes validation and boundary controls as foundational secure development practices (e.g., allowlist validation at input boundaries). ţłÇciteţłéturn8search4ţłéturn8search38ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Broad conceptually, but detection is often framework-aware (routing/middleware patterns differ). Still, the static indicator can be applied via two consistent approaches: framework-specific rule packs and architectural boundary assertions. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search0ţłéturn24search1ţłéturn7search13ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
False positives can be high if tools donÔÇÖt model auth middleware correctly or if authorization is centralized in unusual patterns. False negatives are common where authorization is dynamic (policy engines) or enforced out-of-band (API gateways), unless integrated into the analysis model. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn6search3ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Identify ÔÇťentry pointsÔÇŁ (route handlers, RPC methods, controllers).  
- Require an authentication/authorization precondition (decorator/annotation/middleware call) before privileged sinks (DB write, admin action, secret read). ţłÇciteţłéturn24search0ţłéturn24search1ţłéturn8search4ţłü  
- For ÔÇťprivileged functions,ÔÇŁ enforce that all call paths originate from authenticated contexts (ÔÇťcapability threadingÔÇŁ design). ţłÇciteţłéturn24search0ţłéturn18view0ţłü  

**Examples.**

```javascript
// Express (unsafe): missing auth middleware
app.post("/admin/deleteUser", (req, res) => deleteUser(req.body.id));
```

```javascript
// Express (safer)
app.post("/admin/deleteUser", requireAdmin, (req, res) => deleteUser(req.body.id));
```

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Centralize authentication and authorization enforcement; apply least privilege and separate anonymous vs privileged areas as CWE guidance suggests. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search12ţłéturn24search0ţłéturn8search4ţłü

### Reviewability hotspots as a risk multiplier

**Definition.** Code regions whose structure makes human review and tool reasoning difficult (high cyclomatic complexity, deep nesting, high coupling, large functions, heavy indirection). These are not ÔÇťvulnerabilities,ÔÇŁ but are statistically associated with vulnerability presence and with analyzer misses. ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłéturn6search28ţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłü

**Rationale.** Complexity increases the chance of missing boundary checks, mishandling errors, and creating unanticipated dataflows; it also stresses static analyzersÔÇÖ approximation limits and developer comprehension. ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłü

**Evidence of validation.** Shin & Williams report that complexity metrics can predict vulnerabilities with low false positives but high false negatives. ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłü Microsoft research on vulnerability prediction highlights key challenges such as class imbalance (vulnerabilities are rarer than defects), making prediction difficult and emphasizing that such models are best used as prioritization aids rather than definitive detectors. ţłÇciteţłéturn6search28ţłü

**Cross-language applicability.** Very broad; metrics like cyclomatic complexity, nesting depth, and function length exist across languages and can be computed consistently. ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłéturn6search28ţłü

**False-positive/negative tendencies.**  
By design, this indicator has high ÔÇťfalse positivesÔÇŁ if interpreted as ÔÇťthis is a vulnÔÇŁ; it should be used only as a **prioritization overlay**. False negatives occur because vulnerabilities also exist in simple code (e.g., a single unsafe API call). ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłéturn20view0ţłéturn15view1ţłü

**Static detection heuristics.**

- Set policy thresholds (example starting points): cyclomatic complexity > 15, nesting depth > 4, function length > 150 LOC (tune per codebase). Treat as ÔÇťsecurity review requiredÔÇŁ when combined with sensitive sinks (crypto, auth, deserialization, process execution). ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłéturn15view1ţłü  
- Merge with other findings: prioritize taint-sink findings in high-complexity files/functions first. ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłéturn17view1ţłü  

**Examples (conceptual).**

```python
# Python (reviewability hotspot)
def handle(req):
    if cond1:
        if cond2:
            if cond3:
                # ... many branches ...
                return do_sensitive_thing(req.user_input)
```

```python
# Refactor idea: isolate parsing/validation, isolate sensitive sink, reduce branching
def handle(req):
    user = authenticate(req)
    validated = validate_request(req)
    return do_sensitive_thing(validated)
```

**Mitigation recommendations.**  
Refactor hotspots to isolate security-critical sinks behind small, testable, auditable functions; reduce implicit control flow and consolidate validation/auth checks at boundaries. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search4ţłéturn24search0ţłéturn6search0ţłü

## Comparative table of indicators

The ranges below are **practitioner-oriented expectations** for the indicator family, grounded in published observations of wide variability in static tool precision/recall and benchmark scorecard clusters. They are not universal constants and should be calibrated in each organization by sampling/triage. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view1ţłéturn21view1ţłéturn20view0ţłéturn13academia29ţłü

| Signal name | Detection difficulty | Cross-language coverage | Validation strength | Typical false-positive rate | Typical false-negative rate | Recommended tooling |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Untrusted input Ôćĺ OS command exec | Medium | High | Strong | Medium (10ÔÇô40%) | MediumÔÇôHigh (30ÔÇô70%+) | Taint-capable SAST (CodeQL, Semgrep, Sonar taint), plus strict API denylist rules |
| Untrusted input Ôćĺ dynamic code exec | LowÔÇôMedium | High | Strong | LowÔÇôMedium (5ÔÇô25%) | Medium (20ÔÇô60%) | AST pattern rules + taint (CodeQL/Semgrep); policy bans on `eval`-class sinks |
| Untrusted input Ôćĺ unsafe deserialization | Medium | MediumÔÇôHigh | Strong | Medium (10ÔÇô40%) | MediumÔÇôHigh (30ÔÇô80%) | Platform analyzers (MS rules CA2300*), CodeQL queries, taint rules; migrate away from unsafe serializers |
| Untrusted input Ôćĺ query execution | Medium | High | Strong | Medium (10ÔÇô40%) | MediumÔÇôHigh (30ÔÇô70%+) | Taint-capable SAST; ORM-aware rules; enforce parameterization |
| Untrusted input Ôćĺ filesystem path | Medium | High | Strong | Medium (10ÔÇô40%) | MediumÔÇôHigh (30ÔÇô70%+) | Taint-capable SAST; path canonicalization rules; review of file-serving/extraction code |
| Untrusted input Ôćĺ outbound URL fetch (SSRF) | MediumÔÇôHigh | High | ModerateÔÇôStrong | Medium (15ÔÇô50%) | High (40ÔÇô80%+) | Taint SAST with URL parsing models; HTTPS client config scanners; SSRF policy rules |
| Hardcoded secrets/credentials | Low | High | Strong | Medium (10ÔÇô40%) | Medium (20ÔÇô60%) | Secret scanners + SAST literal/entropy rules; repo pre-receive hooks |
| Weak crypto / insecure randomness | Medium | High | Strong | Medium (10ÔÇô40%) | Medium (30ÔÇô70%) | Crypto-specific rule packs; policy baselines from NIST/IETF; linters |
| TLS certificate validation disabled | Low | High | Strong | Low (<5ÔÇô10%) | Medium (20ÔÇô60%) | Platform analyzers (e.g., CA5359), Node/Python config rules, grep-based enforcement |
| Memory-unsafe primitives / unsafe blocks | High | Medium | Strong (for C/C++) | High (30ÔÇô70%+) | High (40ÔÇô80%+) | C/C++ analyzers (clang tools), CodeQL C/C++ packs, strict API bans, compiler hardening |
| Unchecked errors / swallowed exceptions | Medium | High | ModerateÔÇôStrong | Medium (15ÔÇô50%) | Medium (30ÔÇô70%) | Lints (Go/Java/C#), CWE-focused checkers, ÔÇťfail closedÔÇŁ policy rules |
| Missing authn/authz gates | High | Medium | Strong | High (30ÔÇô70%+) | High (40ÔÇô80%+) | Framework-aware SAST query packs, route/middleware rule sets, architectural guardrail checks |

(ÔÇťValidation strengthÔÇŁ reflects combined grounding in CWE/Top-25 style prioritizations, OWASP/vendor guidance, and measurable tool/benchmark targeting. ţłÇciteţłéturn5search7ţłéturn8search24ţłéturn18view0ţłéturn28search0ţłéturn28search9ţłü)

## Prioritized actionable checklist and lightweight metrics

### Practitioner checklist

1. **Standardize your trust boundary model**: enumerate entry points (HTTP handlers, RPC methods, CLI, file import), and define which inputs are ÔÇťuntrustedÔÇŁ by default; treat boundary validation as a required step. ţłÇciteţłéturn8search4ţłéturn18view0ţłéturn25search0ţłü  
2. **Adopt a small, enforced sink policy** for the riskiest APIs: command execution, dynamic code execution, unsafe deserialization, and ÔÇťtrust-all TLS.ÔÇŁ Make exceptions explicit, reviewed, and local. ţłÇciteţłéturn25search1ţłéturn28search5ţłéturn28search0ţłéturn27search3ţłéturn4search0ţłü  
3. **Prefer safe-by-default APIs** (argv execution, parameterized queries, schema-based parsing, canonicalization checks) and ban known-dangerous calls where practical. ţłÇciteţłéturn5search8ţłéturn8search36ţłéturn8search20ţłéturn28search11ţłéturn28search7ţłü  
4. **Run multi-language SAST with taint capabilities** and calibrate rule packs per language/framework; accept that no single tool excels across all weakness types. ţłÇciteţłéturn18view0ţłéturn17view1ţłéturn7search13ţłéturn7search2ţłü  
5. **Unify outputs in SARIF** so severity, ownership, triage state, and metrics can be aggregated across tools and languages. ţłÇciteţłéturn7search0ţłéturn7search4ţłü  
6. **Treat `unsafe` (Rust) and native/FFI (managed languages) as audit boundaries**: require justification, tests, and focused review. ţłÇciteţłéturn5search5ţłéturn5search1ţłéturn23search0ţłü  
7. **Enforce cryptographic baselines** (approved primitives, secure RNG, TLS configuration) and block insecure primitives in CI. ţłÇciteţłéturn15view3ţłéturn26search13ţłéturn8search19ţłéturn26search0ţłéturn26search2ţłü  
8. **Make failure handling explicit** in security-sensitive pathways: unchecked return values and swallowed exceptions should be treated as high-priority cleanup, especially around auth, crypto, and I/O. ţłÇciteţłéturn24search2ţłéturn24search15ţłéturn24search3ţłéturn28search24ţłü  
9. **Prioritize reviewability hotspots** for security review when they contain sensitive sinks; use complexity only as an overlay, not a ÔÇťvulnerability detector.ÔÇŁ ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłéturn15view1ţłéturn17view1ţłü  
10. **Continuously sample findings** to recalibrate precision/recall expectations for your environment; published work shows wide variance and substantial misses, so on-the-ground calibration is indispensable. ţłÇciteţłéturn20view0ţłéturn21view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn6search3ţłü

### Lightweight metrics to track adoption and effectiveness

These metrics are intentionally low-cost and tool-agnostic; most can be computed from SARIF+repo metadata and a small amount of triage labeling. ţłÇciteţłéturn7search0ţłéturn17view1ţłéturn19view1ţłü

**Adoption metrics**
- **Scan coverage:** % of repos / services shipping code that have SAST enabled per language; % of LOC scanned per release. ţłÇciteţłéturn7search13ţłéturn7search2ţłéturn7search3ţłü  
- **Policy coverage:** count of ÔÇťbanned sinkÔÇŁ rule violations per repo (command execution, unsafe deserialization, trust-all TLS, eval). ţłÇciteţłéturn25search1ţłéturn28search0ţłéturn27search3ţłéturn4search0ţłü  
- **Boundary coverage:** fraction of entry points confirmed to pass through validation/auth middleware (via framework-aware checks). ţłÇciteţłéturn24search0ţłéturn24search1ţłéturn8search4ţłü  

**Effectiveness metrics**
- **Finding quality:** sampled precision = TP / (TP+FP) for top 10 rules by volume; update quarterly. ţłÇciteţłéturn17view1ţłéturn15view1ţłéturn13academia29ţłü  
- **Time-to-triage / time-to-fix:** median days from finding introduction to resolution for high-severity sinks (deserialization, authz, cert validation). ţłÇciteţłéturn28search0ţłéturn24search1ţłéturn27search3ţłü  
- **Recurrence rate:** % of fixed findings that reappear within N releases (indicates missing root cause or insufficient guardrails). ţłÇciteţłéturn13academia29ţłéturn15view1ţłü  

**Risk-reduction proxies (use with caution)**
- **Dangerous-sink density:** number of critical sinks per KLOC, tracked over time (target downward). ţłÇciteţłéturn5search7ţłéturn25search1ţłéturn28search0ţłü  
- **Hotspot exposure:** fraction of sensitive sinks located in high-complexity functions (reducing this indicates improved reviewability around critical code). ţłÇciteţłéturn6search0ţłéturn15view1ţłü  

```mermaid
flowchart LR
  A[Code change] --> B[SAST + secret scan + lint]
  B --> C[SARIF normalization]
  C --> D[Triage sampling]
  D --> E[Policy tuning]
  D --> F[Fix & refactor]
  F --> G[Metrics: precision, MTTR, sink density]
  E --> B
```
9.
# Software Metrics and Structural Signals Predictive of Safe Automated Transformation and Large-Scale Refactoring

## Executive summary

Across decades of empirical software engineering, the most consistently supported predictors of *regression risk* and *nontrivial refactoring effort* are: (a) **change magnitude** (diff ÔÇťsize,ÔÇŁ churn, and diffusion across files/modules); (b) **evolutionary/change coupling** (what tends to co-change, including hidden dependencies not visible in static structure); and (c) **dependency locality / modularity** (how far a change can propagate through dependency reachability and cross-boundary edges). ţłÇciteţłéturn35view3ţłéturn37view0ţłéturn19view1ţłéturn29view3ţłéturn17view3ţłü

A recurring pattern in the evidence base is that **structural information alone is not enough**: studies comparing dependency types find that *logical dependencies (co-change relationships)* can have low overlap with syntactic dependencies and can explain substantial variance in defect proneness beyond what static structure captures. ţłÇciteţłéturn19view1ţłéturn16view3ţłü

For *safe automated transformation* (including AI-assisted rewriting), the research collectively suggests treating refactoring as a **risk-managed, externally verifiable process** rather than a one-shot rewrite. Mature automation systems (semantic patching, large-scale AST-aware migration, translation validation) emphasize (i) constraining change via explicit rules, (ii) preserving maintainability/readability, and (iii) validating results with machine-checkable or test-based evidence. ţłÇciteţłéturn43view1ţłéturn41view8ţłéturn41view4ţłéturn41view0ţłü

Concrete effect sizes and ÔÇťrules of thumbÔÇŁ that recur in the literature include:

- **Churn/change size matters**: relative churn measures correlate strongly with defect density and can discriminate fault-prone artifacts with ~**89%** accuracy in an industrial study. ţłÇciteţłéturn35view3ţłéturn35view0ţłü  
- **Change complexity beyond raw counts helps**: entropy-based change-complexity models reduce fault-prediction error by **13ÔÇô42%** (Ôëł32% on average) versus using modifications alone in multiple OSS systems. ţłÇciteţłéturn36view3ţłü  
- **Effort-aware triage is real**: with ÔÇť20% effortÔÇŁ (review budget defined by churn), an effort-aware model detects ~**35%** of defect-inducing changes on average; in one example project it detects **50%** at the same effort. ţłÇciteţłéturn37view5ţłü  
- **Refactoring is not risk-free**: in one multi-project study, ~**15%** of detected refactorings were associated with later bug fixes; some refactoring types (notably hierarchy-related) were much more likely to be followed by fixes (reported up to **40%** in that dataset). ţłÇciteţłéturn24view0ţłü  
- **Realistic AI code editing tasks are typically multi-location but not enormous**: SWE-bench solutions average editing ~**1.7 files**, ~**3 functions**, and ~**32.8 lines** added/removed, but can also involve nontrivial test dynamics. ţłÇciteţłéturn41view1ţłéturn46view7ţłü  

## Metric definitions and formalizations

This section formalizes the requested dimensionsÔÇöedit distance, change coupling, dependency locality, semantic cohesion, and module boundariesÔÇöin a way that makes them *operational* for automated refactoring and AI-assisted rewriting.

### Edit distance and change magnitude

**Edit distance (string/token level).** A standard formalization treats code as a sequence (characters, tokens) and measures distance as the minimum number of primitive edits to transform one sequence into another (classically: insert, delete, substitute). ţłÇciteţłéturn15search0ţłü

**Edit distance (tree / AST level).** For structured representations, a common formalization is **tree edit distance**: the minimum-cost sequence of node-level operations (insert, delete, relabel/modify; some variants include move) that transforms one ordered labeled tree into another. The classic definition explicitly frames distance as the weighted number of edit operations, with costs forming a metric, and defines distance as the minimum total cost over all edit scripts. ţłÇciteţłéturn44view2ţłéturn44view3ţłü

**Software-evolution ÔÇťedit distanceÔÇŁ proxies used in empirical work.** Many large-scale studies operationalize change magnitude through *diff size* and *churn* features rather than computing exact edit distance:

- **Lines added (LA), lines deleted (LD), churn** (often LA+LD), sometimes normalized by other quantities (ÔÇťrelative churnÔÇŁ). ţłÇciteţłéturn37view0ţłéturn35view3ţłü  
- **Diffusion**: number of files touched (NF) and related spread factors; these are often among the strongest ÔÇťrisk-increasingÔÇŁ factors in just-in-time defect-prediction studies. ţłÇciteţłéturn37view0ţłü  

For automated transformation, these proxies matter because they approximate (i) the surface area for regression, and (ii) the amount of review/verification work required.

### Change coupling

**Core idea.** *Change coupling* (also ÔÇťlogical coupling,ÔÇŁ ÔÇťevolutionary couplingÔÇŁ) measures implicit dependency between items A and B by how often they change together across version-control transactions. A widely used rationale is: if entities co-changed in the past, they are likely to co-change again (and thus should be analyzed together). ţłÇciteţłéturn16view6ţłü

**Operationalizations.**

1. **Co-change frequency / support (association-rule view).** A simple measure is support count: how often a pair (or set) appears in the same transaction. Some tools convert this into association rules with **support** and **confidence**, i.e., support of \(x_1 \Rightarrow x_2\) equals frequency of \(x_1 \cup x_2\), and confidence equals \(\text{frq}(x_1 \cup x_2)/\text{frq}(x_1)\). ţłÇciteţłéturn17view0ţłü  

2. **Support-count thresholds as ÔÇťstrength.ÔÇŁ** In a classic impact-analysis tool, the authors explicitly argue that a **support count of ~10** represents ÔÇťrather strongÔÇŁ evolutionary coupling and is relatively reusable across projects (while noting normalization considerations). ţłÇciteţłéturn16view1ţłü  

3. **Fine-grained logical coupling.** Some research argues that commit-transaction granularity is too coarse (all files in a commit contribute equally), and proposes measures that weight co-change by *how much* or *what kind* of change occurred. ţłÇciteţłéturn16view6ţłü  

4. **Change sequences across releases.** Earlier work proposed analyzing ÔÇťchange sequencesÔÇŁ (release numbers where a moduleÔÇÖs version changes) and then seeking long shared subsequences as evidence of coupling; longer shared subsequences are argued to imply higher probability of real dependency. ţłÇciteţłéturn18view1ţłéturn18view2ţłü  

### Dependency locality

Here ÔÇťdependency localityÔÇŁ is treated as a *design and evolution property*: changes and dependencies should mostly stay within a module/cluster; cross-boundary relationships are costly because they increase change propagation, coordination needs, and verification radius.

**A graph-based formalization.** Let a system be a directed graph \(G=(V,E)\) over entities (files, packages, classes, functions). Let a module partition \(\Pi=\{M_1,\dots,M_k\}\) split \(V\). Basic locality measures include:

- **Boundary-crossing ratio:** \(|\{(u,v)\in E : u\in M_i, v\in M_j, i\neq j\}|/|E|\)  
- **Locality-weighted cut:** same numerator but weighted by edge types (imports, calls, shared state, etc.)  

**Propagation-based locality (Design Structure Matrix lineage).** A rigorous and widely cited approach computes reachability in the dependency graph and summarizes how widely a change can spread:

- Build a dependency matrix; compute a **visibility matrix** capturing direct and indirect dependency reachability (via matrix powers and summation).  
- Define **fan-out visibility** and **fan-in visibility** from the visibility matrix.  
- Define system-level **Propagation Cost** as the average proportion of elements that could be affected by a change to one element. ţłÇciteţłéturn29view3ţłéturn29view4ţłü  

**Cluster-aware locality.** A complementary measure assigns low cost to dependencies within clusters and higher cost across clusters, comparing designs against an ÔÇťidealized modular formÔÇŁ (tightly connected clusters, minimal cross-cluster edges). ţłÇciteţłéturn29view4ţłü  

**Evolutionary locality via co-change.** Recent work adapts DSM-style propagation ideas to *co-change graphs* by constructing a weighted co-change matrix and using matrix exponential to dampen long walks, yielding weighted propagation/clustering costs that track how co-changes propagate through the system over time. ţłÇciteţłéturn30view2ţłü  

### Semantic cohesion

**Concept.** Semantic cohesion measures whether the elements within a module/class ÔÇťbelong togetherÔÇŁ conceptually (shared vocabulary, shared topics, consistent intent), which affects refactorability and auditability: cohesive modules localize reasoning, review, and invariants.

**A classic formalization: Conceptual Cohesion of Classes (C3).** One approach extracts identifiers/comments as a text corpus, embeds methods as vectors (via LSI), computes conceptual similarity as cosine similarity, and defines a class cohesion metric in \([0,1]\). The paper gives explicit definitions for method similarity and average similarity within a class. ţłÇciteţłéturn20view0ţłéturn20view1ţłü  

**Semantic coupling as boundary signal.** Empirical work comparing coupling measures finds that semantic coupling can capture relationships not visible in structural call relations, because interactions may be ÔÇťencapsulated in the source code vocabulary.ÔÇŁ ţłÇciteţłéturn21view0ţłéturn21view3ţłü  

**Topic-model formulations for refactoring recommendations.** A refactoring-recommendation technique models methods as documents and uses **Relational Topic Models (RTM)** (topics + relationships) to recommend Move Method targets, explicitly aiming to reduce low cohesion / high coupling scenarios. ţłÇciteţłéturn45view0ţłéturn45view3ţłü  

### Module boundaries

**Foundational criterion: information hiding.** A seminal modularity argument is that modules should be organized around ÔÇťsecretsÔÇŁ (design decisions likely to change), so that changes can be confined to one module. The paper contrasts decompositions and shows cases where, under an information-hiding decomposition, a change is confined to a single module that hides representation details. ţłÇciteţłéturn39view2ţłü  

**Boundary quality as an empirical discrepancy problem.** A practical, evolution-based interpretation is: boundaries are good when *structural coupling* and *change coupling* agree (what depends should co-evolve, and what should be independent should not repeatedly co-change). Discrepancies can reveal ÔÇťmodularity violationsÔÇŁ and design erosion. ţłÇciteţłéturn17view1ţłéturn17view3ţłü  

## Empirical evidence linking metrics to refactorability, automated transformability, and regression risk

Empirical studies rarely measure ÔÇťrefactorabilityÔÇŁ directly; instead they use proxies: defect proneness, prediction accuracy, impact-scope correctness, maintenance effort, developer perception alignment, ability to localize redesign, or success of automated refactoring at scale. This section emphasizes effect sizes and thresholds when reported.

### Comparative study table

| Study (first author) | Year | Dataset / context | Metrics / signals | Methods | Key findings and effect sizes (where reported) |
|---|---:|---|---|---|---|
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","David L. Parnas","software modularity pioneer"]ţłü** | 1972 | Conceptual design exemplar (KWIC) | Module boundaries via information hiding; change confinement | Comparative decomposition argument | Information-hiding modularization confines certain representation changes to a single module (ÔÇťentirely hiddenÔÇŁ), improving independent development and comprehension; no numeric effect sizes (design rationale). ţłÇciteţłéturn39view2ţłéturn39view0ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","William G. Griswold","software refactoring researcher"]ţłü** & **ţłÇentityţłé["people","David Notkin","program analysis researcher"]ţłü** | 1993 | Restructuring prototype + conceptual examples | ÔÇťStructural degradationÔÇŁ as need for consistent nonlocal change; transformation safety constraints | Model + prototype; formalizes when transformations abort (side effects, multiple defs) | Frames nonlocal change as key characteristic of degradation and motivates meaning-preserving restructuring; emphasizes explicit validation stages and conditions that prevent unsafe rewrites; limited effect-size reporting in the excerpted material. ţłÇciteţłéturn43view5ţłéturn42search3ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Harald C. Gall","software evolution researcher"]ţłü** | 1998 | Telecom Switching System (~10M LOC, thousands of files) | Logical coupling via shared change sequences/subsequences | Change Sequence Analysis + Change Report Analysis | Longer shared subsequences imply higher probability of coupling; intended to guide restructuring; no explicit quantitative thresholds beyond this principle in the excerpted sections. ţłÇciteţłéturn18view0ţłéturn18view2ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Thomas Zimmermann","software engineering researcher"]ţłü** | 2005 | Eclipse plugin change history | Evolutionary coupling rules; support-count thresholds; precision/recall | Association rule mining + tool evaluation (ROSE) | Argues support count is easier for developers; suggests support count ~10 is ÔÇťrather strongÔÇŁ coupling. Reports precision Ôëł0.30 and recall Ôëł0.34 under evaluated settings; feedback Ôëł0.64 (suggestions returned in ~2/3 queries). ţłÇciteţłéturn16view1ţłéturn16view2ţłéturn16view0ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Marcelo Cataldo","software process researcher"]ţłü** | 2009 | Two industrial projects (8 years, 154 devs); customer-reported defects | Syntactic deps vs logical deps vs workflow deps; clustering of logical deps | Logistic regression (odds ratios); network measures | Many syntactic dependencies were not logical dependencies (74.3% in project A; 97.3% in project B). Logical dependencies explain substantial variance and complement syntactic. Workflow-dependency odds ratios reported (e.g., log-unit increase Ôćĺ odds ├Ś2.011 in project A; ├Ś6.527 in project B). ţłÇciteţłéturn19view1ţłéturn19view2ţłéturn16view3ţłü |
| (Same first author as above) | 2011 | Hadoop + Eclipse JDT; releases + modification requests | Modularity violations = recurring discrepancies between structural and change-coupling impact scopes | Threshold sweeping + frequent-pattern mining | The tool detects confirmed violations on average ~6 releases early in Hadoop and ~5 in Eclipse JDT. A large share are ÔÇťhiddenÔÇŁ (co-change without explicit dependency): 66/152 confirmed in Hadoop; 25/161 in Eclipse. ţłÇciteţłéturn17view2ţłéturn17view3ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Alan MacCormack","software architecture researcher"]ţłü** | 2004ÔÇô2006 | DSM analysis of Linux vs Mozilla; Mozilla redesign | Propagation Cost; Clustered Cost; structural modularity | Static call extraction Ôćĺ DSM; longitudinal & cross-sectional comparison | Redesign reduced propagation cost from ~15ÔÇô18% down to ~2ÔÇô6% and clustered cost by an order of magnitude (trend claim). A specific comparison reports propagation cost 2.78% (Mozilla after redesign) vs 5.65% (Linux 2.1.88) at similar file counts. ţłÇciteţłéturn28view0ţłéturn28view1ţłéturn29view3ţłéturn29view4ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Nachiappan Nagappan","software analytics researcher"]ţłü** | 2005 | Windows Server 2003 SP1 binaries | Relative churn metrics; defect density | Regression + discriminant analysis | Reports R┬▓ Ôëł 0.811 for regression fit and strong Pearson/Spearman correlations (e.g., Pearson 0.889, Spearman 0.929). Discriminates fault-prone vs not with ~89% accuracy (test data reported up to ~90.1%). ţłÇciteţłéturn35view3ţłéturn35view0ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Ahmed E. Hassan","software evolution researcher"]ţłü** | 2009 | NetBSD, FreeBSD, OpenBSD, Postgres, KDE, KOffice (5-year windows) | Change complexity via entropy (HCM variants) | Statistical linear regression; prediction error comparison + t-tests | Entropy-based models outperform ÔÇťmodifications-only,ÔÇŁ reducing prediction error by ~13ÔÇô42% (Ôëł32% avg) in stated comparison. A decay variant can outperform ÔÇťprior faultsÔÇŁ predictors by ~15ÔÇô38% depending on system (significance varies). ţłÇciteţłéturn36view3ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Hirotaka Kamei","software quality researcher"]ţłü** | 2013 | Multiple OSS + commercial systems; JIT quality assurance | NF, relative churn (LA/LT, LT/NF), AGE, FIX etc. | Logistic/effort-aware models; AUC; effort-based lift charts | Identifies NF and relative churn as key risk-increasing factors; AGE risk-decreasing (recently changed Ôćĺ higher risk). With 20% effort, effort-aware prediction detects ~35% of defect-inducing changes on average; one example reports 50% at 20% effort. Best ROI often at 10% effort (project-dependent). ţłÇciteţłéturn37view0ţłéturn37view5ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Jean-R├ęmy Falleri","program transformation researcher"]ţłü** | 2014 | 12,792 file-pair differencing scenarios; Jenkins + jQuery; manual rating dataset ŕ│Áŕ░ť | AST edit scripts; script-size minimization; move detection | Manual study (agreement) + large-scale automatic comparisons | Manual rating: reports substantial agreement and kappa 0.426 for one question; dataset released. Automatic evaluation: GumTree better than ChangeDistiller in many casesÔÇöe.g., at fine-grained JDT AST level, ÔÇťscript sizeÔÇŁ better in 80.97% of cases and mappings better in 65.49%. ţłÇciteţłéturn34view0ţłéturn34view0ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Beat Fluri","software change mining researcher"]ţłü** | 2007 | 1,064 manually classified changes; 219 revisions; 3 OSS projects (8 methods) | Fine-grained AST edit scripts; change taxonomy | Benchmarking tree differencing | Reports 45% improvement vs baseline algorithm and mean absolute percentage error of 34% vs minimum conforming edit script; focuses on enabling risk-aware evolution tooling. ţłÇciteţłéturn32view4ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Roland Benkoczi","software modularity researcher"]ţłü** | 2018 | GNU Octave evolution across years/phases | Co-change DSM; weighted propagation cost via matrix exponential | Time-sliced mining + DSM analytics | Defines co-change DSM with off-diagonal entries = times committed together; weighted propagation = sum of matrix exponential entries; shows measure distinguishes dependency structures that plain propagation cost cannot (example numeric contrasts provided). ţłÇciteţłéturn30view2ţłéturn30view1ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Gabriele Bavota","software maintenance researcher"]ţłü** | 2013 | ArgoUML, JHotDraw, jEdit; participants + project devs | Structural vs dynamic vs semantic vs logical coupling; MoJoFM modularization distance | Human study + statistical testing | Finds semantic coupling better matches developersÔÇÖ perceived coupling; reports statistically significant differences (p < 0.01) with medium/high effect sizes in some comparisons (e.g., highly coupled classes). ţłÇciteţłéturn21view0ţłéturn21view3ţłü |
| (Same first author as above) | 2012 | Multiple OSS systems; refactorings across releases; SZZ linkage | Refactoring types; bug-fix induction rate | Ref-Finder + manual validation + SZZ-style linking | Reports overall ~15% of refactorings associated with later bug fixes; some refactoring types much higher (e.g., hierarchy-related reported 40% in their data). ţłÇciteţłéturn24view0ţłéturn24view2ţłü |
| (Same first author as above; Methodbook) | 2013ÔÇô2014 | Multiple OSS; dev evaluation on jEdit and JFreeChart | Structural + textual (RTM) for Move Method; coupling/cohesion proxies; human scoring | Comparative tool study + Wilcoxon + CliffÔÇÖs delta | Methodbook recommends moving ~2% of methods vs another tool at ~4% in their evaluated corpus. External-dev study reports significant comparisons with CliffÔÇÖs delta magnitudes (e.g., d = -0.61 Large; -0.37 Medium; -0.22 Small for a jEdit comparison) with p < 0.0001. ţłÇciteţłéturn46view0ţłéturn46view2ţłéturn45view0ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Julia Lawall","program transformation researcher"]ţłü** | 2018 | Linux kernel automation practice | SmPL semantic patches; maintainability-preserving transformation | Longitudinal/qualitative + tool-design analysis | Defines SmPL structure (rules/hunks; concrete syntax, metavariables; ÔÇťÔÇŽÔÇŁ). Notes design requirement that transformed code retain original structure (comments/whitespace) for maintainability; argues correctness is managed by embedding invariants in semantic patches and developer review of false positives. ţłÇciteţłéturn43view0ţłéturn43view1ţłéturn43view2ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","George C. Necula","program verification researcher"]ţłü** | 2000 | GNU C compiler optimizations; realistic programs (incl. GCC itself, Linux kernel) | Translation validation (per-compilation checking) | Validator infrastructure based on equivalence checking | Claims translation validation infrastructure can be implemented with effort comparable to a compiler pass; intended to isolate transformation errors during testing/maintenance, supporting ÔÇťcheck each compilationÔÇŁ goal. ţłÇciteţłéturn41view4ţłéturn41view6ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Hyrum K. Wright","large-scale refactoring researcher"]ţłü** | 2013 | Large internal C++ codebase at ţłÇentityţłé["company","Google","technology company"]ţłü | Semantically-aware large-scale refactoring; AST matching & rewrite | MapReduce-parallel compilation/AST extraction + rewrite | Describes AST matcher producing textual-level transformation instructions; reports practical ability to migrate bulk code while repeatedly rerunning on evolving codebase (experience report; effect sizes not in excerpt). ţłÇciteţłéturn41view8ţłéturn41view9ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","John Yang","machine learning researcher"]ţłü** (SWE-bench paper team; first author in the PDF) | 2024 | SWE-bench: 2,294 real issues/PRs across 12 Python repos from ţłÇentityţłé["company","GitHub","code hosting platform"]ţłü | Multi-file edit scope; edited files/functions/lines; test transitions | Benchmark construction + evaluation framework | Benchmark targets ÔÇťrevisions in multiple locations of a large codebase.ÔÇŁ Reference solutions average ~1.7 files, ~3 functions, ~32.8 lines edited. Table-level repo-specific means show variability (e.g., lines edited). ţłÇciteţłéturn41view0ţłéturn41view1ţłéturn46view7ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Qian Guo","software engineering researcher"]ţłü** | 2024 | Code review/refinement datasets (CodeReview + new datasets); evaluates ChatGPT settings | Prompt + temperature sensitivity; error taxonomies | 25 prompt├Śtemperature combos; quantitative + qualitative analysis | Reports need to avoid data contamination, builds ÔÇťCodeReview-New.ÔÇŁ Root-cause categories include ÔÇťmodel fallacyÔÇŁ vs vague comments; provides underperformance breakdown (e.g., majority due to lack of domain knowledge among incorrect answers in a cited table excerpt). ţłÇciteţłéturn45view4ţłéturn46view4ţłü |
| **ţłÇentityţłé["people","Nils Peitek","software comprehension researcher"]ţłü** | 2026 | 230 Java snippets; 5 iterative refactor rounds; different prompting strategies | DiffParser change-type distributions (rename/syntax-only/code change, etc.) | Large-scale experiment + change analysis | Observes convergence trend: early iterations dominated by systematic renaming; later iterations concentrate into smaller code changes and minor adjustments, indicating stabilization behavior under iterative refactoring. ţłÇciteţłéturn45view7ţłéturn46view5ţłéturn46view6ţłü |

### What the evidence implies for each metric

**Edit distance / churn / diffusion Ôćĺ regression risk and verification cost.** Multiple studies show that larger and more dispersed changes are associated with increased defect proneness or reduced cost-effectiveness of inspection. The strongest ÔÇťportableÔÇŁ lesson is not a universal threshold of ÔÇťN lines,ÔÇŁ but that *relative measures* and normalization (e.g., churn normalized by size) predict better than absolute counts, and that coupling with diffusion (NF) matters. ţłÇciteţłéturn35view3ţłéturn35view0ţłéturn37view0ţłü

**Change coupling Ôćĺ hidden dependency discovery and impact-scope completeness.** Change coupling repeatedly appears as (i) a guide to what must be changed together, and (ii) a signal of boundary problems when it contradicts structural dependency expectations. Evidence includes low overlaps between syntactic and logical dependencies and concrete defect-risk odds ratios tied to dependency structures. ţłÇciteţłéturn19view1ţłéturn16view3ţłéturn17view3ţłü

**Dependency locality / modularity Ôćĺ ability to localize redesign and reduce propagation.** DSM-derived propagation cost changes dramatically after redesign, consistent with the idea that modularity improvements reduce the average ÔÇťblast radiusÔÇŁ of a change. Such reductions are large enough (e.g., ~15ÔÇô18% to ~2ÔÇô6%) to be operationally meaningful for safe automated transformation planning (smaller expected verification scope per change). ţłÇciteţłéturn28view0ţłéturn29view3ţłéturn29view4ţłü

**Semantic cohesion / semantic coupling Ôćĺ auditability and ÔÇťhuman-aligned boundaries.ÔÇŁ** Human studies suggest semantic coupling better matches developersÔÇÖ mental models than purely structural coupling in many cases, which is relevant when building AI tools intended to be auditable and reviewable by humans (the model should surface the relationships humans expect). ţłÇciteţłéturn21view0ţłéturn21view3ţłü

**Module boundaries Ôćĺ change confinement and evolvability.** The core ÔÇťinformation hidingÔÇŁ claim is that when modules hide likely-to-change decisions, changes remain localized. Empirical toolwork operationalizes boundary quality via discrepancies between expected structural evolution and observed co-change clusters. ţłÇciteţłéturn39view2ţłéturn17view3ţłü

## Measurement methods and key tools

This section focuses on *how* to compute the metrics robustly at scale and what measurement pitfalls matter for automated transformation and AI-assisted editing.

### Computing edit distance and change magnitude

1. **Line-level diff and churn** is cheap and common. For large-scale risk models, LA/LD and churn (LA+LD) are standard, often normalized for comparability (e.g., dividing by LT, total lines) and used as effort proxies. ţłÇciteţłéturn37view0ţłéturn35view3ţłü  

2. **Tree/AST differencing** improves semantic alignment: rather than ÔÇťadd/delete line,ÔÇŁ AST differencing yields edit scripts closer to developer intent, including move actions. GumTree reports evaluation metrics tied to mapping quality and script size across thousands of scenarios, and ChangeDistiller provides a benchmark showing improved accuracy relative to earlier methods. ţłÇciteţłéturn34view0ţłéturn32view4ţłü  

   Practical note: minimum edit scripts with move actions are NP-hard in general; tool implementations therefore use heuristics and hybrid approaches. ţłÇciteţłéturn34view1ţłü  

3. **Choice of representation matters**: even the GumTree evaluation explicitly distinguishes coarser ASTs (ÔÇťCDÔÇŁ / ChangeDistiller-like) and finer-grained ASTs (Eclipse JDT), and performance comparisons shift with granularity. ţłÇciteţłéturn34view0ţłü  

### Computing change coupling

1. **Transaction reconstruction** is a core method issue: systems with true atomic commits (e.g., modern DVCS) are easier than CVS-like histories. Tools and papers explicitly define transactions as atomic commits and use heuristics when needed. ţłÇciteţłéturn17view0ţłéturn16view0ţłü  

2. **Association-rule measures (support/confidence)** are common, and some systems argue for support count as a developer-friendly strength measure with reusable thresholds (e.g., ~10). ţłÇciteţłéturn16view1ţłü  

3. **Fine-grained change data** can improve coupling measurement compared to coarse commit-level increments by weighting how entities changed. ţłÇciteţłéturn16view6ţłü  

### Computing dependency locality and module boundary signals

1. **Static dependency extraction Ôćĺ graphs/DSMs.** One DSM approach builds dependencies from static call extraction at source-file granularity and then computes visibility/propagation and cluster-aware measures; the study explicitly selected a commercial static analysis tool for call extraction (chosen for multi-language support and automation). ţłÇciteţłéturn29view2ţłéturn29view3ţłü  

2. **Propagation cost via reachability (visibility matrix).** Propagation cost summarizes average reachabilityÔÇöhow many nodes can be affected by a change. This is directly aligned with safe transformation planning because it approximates verification scope. ţłÇciteţłéturn29view3ţłéturn29view4ţłü  

3. **Boundary discrepancy diagnostics.** If structural coupling predicts impact scope but co-change predicts a different and recurring scope, recurring discrepancies can be mined as modularity violations; thresholds are tuned empirically (the cited tool varies weight thresholds and support/confidence thresholds). ţłÇciteţłéturn17view1ţłéturn17view0ţłü  

4. **Co-change modularity over time** can be captured by co-change DSMs and matrix exponentialÔÇôbased propagation measures, which dampen long walks and distinguish dependency structures that a plain transitive-closure propagation cost cannot. ţłÇciteţłéturn30view2ţłéturn30view1ţłü  

### Computing semantic cohesion

1. **IR-based semantic cohesion (LSI)**: extract identifiers/comments; build corpus; embed methods; compute cosine similarity; aggregate into cohesion measures like C3. ţłÇciteţłéturn20view0ţłéturn20view1ţłü  

2. **Topic models + relations (RTM)**: treat methods as documents and model link probabilities between documents in a logistic-regression link function over topic distributions (Hadamard product); used to recommend Move Method targets. ţłÇciteţłéturn45view3ţłéturn45view0ţłü  

## Interactions among metrics

The strongest practical signals emerge not from any single metric but from **metric interactions**ÔÇöespecially mismatches between structural and evolutionary views.

### Structural coupling ├Ś change coupling

A high-impact pattern is **low overlap between syntactic dependencies and logical dependencies**. In one industrial comparison, most syntactic dependencies were not mirrored by logical dependencies (large differences in overlap percentages), and logical dependencies explained more variance in failure proneness than syntactic ones. ţłÇciteţłéturn19view1ţłéturn16view3ţłü  

This mismatch has two transformation-relevant interpretations:

- **Hidden dependencies**: code that ÔÇťshouldnÔÇÖtÔÇŁ depend structurally may still co-evolve, implying semantic or process coupling that automation must respect (e.g., encoded conventions, shared protocol decisions). ţłÇciteţłéturn17view3ţłéturn16view3ţłü  
- **Boundary erosion**: repeated cross-module co-change suggests boundaries are misdrawn or have eroded; large-scale refactoring is often warranted to realign structure with evolution. ţłÇciteţłéturn17view3ţłéturn39view2ţłü  

### Change magnitude ├Ś coupling ├Ś locality

Large edits are not uniformly risky; risk spikes when large edits are **diffuse** or touch **high-coupling / high-propagation** areas:

- JIT models highlight **NF** (number of files) and **relative churn** as key risk-increasing factors, indicating that magnitude and diffusion interact. ţłÇciteţłéturn37view0ţłéturn37view5ţłü  
- Propagation cost (reachability) provides a structural analog: a change to a high fan-out-visible element has large expected impact scope even if the patch is small. ţłÇciteţłéturn29view3ţłéturn29view4ţłü  

### Semantic cohesion ├Ś boundary selection

Semantic couplingÔÇÖs better alignment with developer perception suggests that *semantically coherent clusters* may be more reviewable/auditable units than syntactic containers alone, especially for AI-assisted editing where the reviewer must validate intent, not just compilation. ţłÇciteţłéturn21view0ţłéturn21view3ţłü  

### Diagram: structural dependencies, co-change edges, and a boundary cut

```mermaid
graph LR
  %% Nodes are code entities; solid edges are structural deps; dotted edges are co-change.
  subgraph M1[Module A]
    A1[File/Class A1]
    A2[File/Class A2]
  end
  subgraph M2[Module B]
    B1[File/Class B1]
    B2[File/Class B2]
  end

  A1 -->|call/import| A2
  B1 -->|call/import| B2

  A2 -->|call/import| B1
  A1 -.->|co-change| B2
  A2 -.->|co-change| B2
```

Interpreting this diagram operationally: cross-boundary structural edges (A2ÔćĺB1) increase propagation cost; persistent cross-boundary co-change edges (A1ÔćöB2, A2ÔćöB2) can indicate hidden dependencies or modularity violations that should influence how an automated refactoring chunks work and how verification is scoped. ţłÇciteţłéturn29view3ţłéturn17view3ţłéturn19view1ţłü  

## Implications for AI-assisted code rewriting

This section maps the metric landscape to concrete design choices for AI-assisted editing when the goals include **safety**, **auditability**, and **evolvability**ÔÇönot only functional correctness.

### Chunking and context selection guided by coupling and boundaries

**Why chunking must be dependency-aware.** SWE-bench explicitly frames repository-scale editing as multi-location changes in a large codebase and reports that reference fixes on average touch multiple files/functions. ţłÇciteţłéturn41view1ţłéturn41view0ţłü  

A practical AI system should therefore treat ÔÇťwhat context to includeÔÇŁ as an algorithmic decision, using:

- **Top-N change-coupled neighbors** of edited entities (evolutionary coupling). This is directly aligned with ROSE-style recommendations and with the finding that logical dependencies can dominate in explanatory power. ţłÇciteţłéturn16view2ţłéturn16view3ţłéturn19view1ţłü  
- **Boundary-aware scoping**: prioritize keeping an AI edit within a module boundary unless the coupling graph indicates cross-boundary co-change is requiredÔÇöbecause cross-boundary edits amplify verification scope and reviewer burden. ţłÇciteţłéturn39view2ţłéturn17view3ţłéturn37view0ţłü  

### Prompting strategies as a controllable risk factor

Empirical AI-refinement work shows performance depends strongly on **prompt and temperature**, and it explicitly builds new datasets to mitigate training contamination risk. ţłÇciteţłéturn45view4ţłéturn45view5ţłü  

Root causes for incorrect outputs include ambiguous/vague human comments (model cannot infer exact change) and ÔÇťmodel fallacyÔÇŁ cases where comments are clear to humans yet the model failsÔÇösuggesting that safety and auditability require **explicit, structured intent specifications** (e.g., invariants, pre/postconditions, do-not-change constraints). ţłÇciteţłéturn46view4ţłéturn45view4ţłü  

### Using edit distance to enforce ÔÇťminimal changeÔÇŁ and staged refactoring

Large-scale transformations can be decomposed into smaller, reviewable steps. Two empirical lines support this:

- Defect and QA studies show that change size/diffusion is risk-increasing, and that cost-effectiveness peaks at relatively small inspection budgets (often 10ÔÇô20% effort), reinforcing that a refactoring pipeline should produce **small, high-confidence deltas** first. ţłÇciteţłéturn37view5ţłéturn35view3ţłü  
- Iterative refactoring experiments observe a stabilization pattern: early iterations dominated by systematic renaming, later iterations converge toward smaller changesÔÇösuggesting that ÔÇťmulti-pass refactoringÔÇŁ can be a viable control strategy for AI systems (rename pass Ôćĺ structure pass Ôćĺ cleanup pass). ţłÇciteţłéturn46view5ţłéturn46view6ţłü  

### Verification: from test-only to multi-layer validation

**Tests remain central** in real-world benchmarks: SWE-bench uses test outcomes as the execution ground truth, and reports test-transition counts (failÔćĺpass, passÔćĺpass) and change statistics by repository. ţłÇciteţłéturn46view7ţłéturn41view0ţłü  

But for large-scale refactoring and automated transformation, tests alone are often insufficient for auditability/evolvability. The mature automation literature suggests layering:

- **Rule-constrained transformations (semantic patches).** SmPL semantic patches embed developer invariants directly, preserve code structure/readability, and give developers control over false positivesÔÇösupporting auditability at scale. ţłÇciteţłéturn43view0ţłéturn43view1ţłéturn43view2ţłü  
- **Semantically-aware, compiler-grade rewriting.** Large-scale tools extract ASTs and use matchers to produce edits, enabling systematic migrations and repeated re-runs on evolving codebases. ţłÇciteţłéturn41view8ţłéturn41view9ţłü  
- **Translation validation / equivalence checking where feasible.** Translation validation proposes ÔÇťcheck each compilationÔÇŁ rather than proving the compiler correct, arguing practical infrastructure costs comparable to a compiler pass; conceptually, this is a blueprint for validating transformation steps (including refactoring) rather than trusting the transformer. ţłÇciteţłéturn41view4ţłéturn41view6ţłü  

### Provenance and audit artifacts as first-class outputs

A key observation from tool-centric research is that *understandability* and *maintainability* constraints must be preserved (e.g., retaining whitespace/comments in Coccinelle-generated patches). For AI tools, this generalizes into a provenance contract:

- Store the modelÔÇÖs ÔÇťedit intentÔÇŁ boundaries (what was supposed to change, what must not change). ţłÇciteţłéturn43view1ţłéturn39view2ţłü  
- Store the metric deltas: churn, diffusion, impacted dependency scopes, and coupling-based neighbor sets used for context. ţłÇciteţłéturn37view0ţłéturn29view3ţłéturn16view1ţłü  
- Store verification evidence: tests run, static checks, and any equivalence arguments/validators used. ţłÇciteţłéturn41view0ţłéturn41view4ţłü  

### Diagram: a metric-driven, verification-centered AI refactoring pipeline

```mermaid
flowchart TD
  A[Extract metrics\n(churn/edit size, diffusion,\nco-change, propagation cost,\nsemantic cohesion)] --> B[Plan change\n(chunk by module boundaries + coupling)]
  B --> C[Generate transformation\n(LLM edit or rule-based codemod)]
  C --> D[Verify\n(build + tests + static analysis\n+ targeted checks on coupled areas)]
  D -->|fail| B
  D --> E[Audit package\n(diff + rationale + metrics + provenance\n+ reproducible commands)]
  E --> F[Human review + merge]
  F --> G[Post-merge monitoring\n(regressions, rollback path)]
```

This flow is directly motivated by: (i) churn/diffusion-driven risk and effort-aware evaluation; (ii) discrepancy and hidden-dependency findings; and (iii) established transformation systems that privilege validation and maintainability. ţłÇciteţłéturn37view5ţłéturn17view3ţłéturn41view4ţłéturn43view1ţłü  

### Practical recommendations table

| Target | Recommendation | Metric triggers / thresholds (from literature when available) | Rationale tied to safety/auditability/evolvability |
|---|---|---|---|
| Engineers | Prefer refactoring PRs that are **small and staged**, separating semantics-preserving refactors from behavior changes | Use churn (LA+LD) and NF as review-budget proxies; effort-aware results suggest ROI peaks often at ~10ÔÇô20% effort cutoffs (project-dependent) | Smaller deltas reduce regression surface area and make code review and rollback more reliable. ţłÇciteţłéturn37view5ţłéturn35view3ţłü |
| Engineers | Treat **cross-module edits** as ÔÇťhigh ceremonyÔÇŁ changes | Use propagation-like reachability or boundary-crossing ratios; large redesign reduced propagation cost dramatically, showing how impactful boundary work is | Cross-boundary edits multiply impacted invariants and required review scope. ţłÇciteţłéturn29view3ţłéturn28view0ţłü |
| Engineers | Mine **co-change neighbors** before starting a refactor; update them in the same change or add explicit contracts/tests | A support count around ~10 is argued as ÔÇťstrongÔÇŁ in one tool; tune per repo | Prevent partial migrations that leave latent inconsistencies. ţłÇciteţłéturn16view1ţłéturn16view2ţłü |
| Engineers | Use modularity-violation discovery to prioritize architectural refactors | Violations can be detected ~5ÔÇô6 releases before refactor; large fractions can be ÔÇťhiddenÔÇŁ (no explicit deps) | Targets high-leverage architectural debt that drives repeated nonlocal change. ţłÇciteţłéturn17view2ţłéturn17view3ţłü |
| Engineers | Increase semantic cohesion before large refactors (rename/structure cleanup) | Track semantic cohesion (e.g., C3-style) or semantic coupling; validate naming/doc quality | Higher cohesion improves understandability and reduces brittle cross-cutting edits. ţłÇciteţłéturn20view0ţłéturn21view0ţłü |
| AI tool designers | Use **metric-driven context selection** rather than ÔÇťcurrent file onlyÔÇŁ | Include top change-coupled neighbors; include boundary/interface files; include tests in impacted scope | Addresses multi-location nature of realistic tasks; reduces omission errors. ţłÇciteţłéturn41view1ţłéturn16view2ţłéturn19view1ţłü |
| AI tool designers | Constrain edits toward **minimal AST/script changes** and produce structured edit scripts | Use AST differencing (e.g., GumTree); compare generated patch vs minimal-edit baselines | Improves provenance, reviewability, and reduces unintended drift. ţłÇciteţłéturn34view0ţłéturn35view3ţłü |
| AI tool designers | Prefer **rule-based / semantic-patch** transformations for repetitive or safety-critical patterns; fall back to LLM only where rules fail | SmPL-style patches encode invariants and preserve code layout for maintainability | Maximizes auditability, reproducibility, and controlled false-positive rates. ţłÇciteţłéturn43view0ţłéturn43view1ţłü |
| AI tool designers | Make verification multi-layer: tests + static rules + equivalence-style validation where feasible | Translation validation shows per-transformation checking can be practical in compiler setting | Compensates for model fallacies and ambiguous intent; improves safety claims. ţłÇciteţłéturn41view4ţłéturn46view4ţłü |
| AI tool designers | Log and expose **prompting and settings** (prompt templates, temperature) as part of provenance | Refactoring/refinement outcomes depend on prompts/settings; datasets attempt to avoid contamination | Supports reproducibility and audit trails in regulated environments. ţłÇciteţłéturn45view4ţłü |
| AI tool designers | Support **iterative refactoring** with stabilization gates | Iterative study shows early rename-heavy passes and later stabilization into smaller edits | Allows controlled convergence; each iteration has smaller diff and clearer review. ţłÇciteţłéturn46view5ţłéturn46view6ţłü |

## Limitations and open problems

### Limitations in the current evidence base
